    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="88a9b37dff7248308171f7f5746194b0">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '01f0e391-b59c-40ef-b888-fd4251a740f3'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

</li>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d187944537" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            <p>
Nowadays, hard carbon is one of the most promising anode materials for sodium-ion batteries. However, the Na-storage performance of hard carbon varies for different precursors and synthetic processes due to disparities in the dopant content, dimensions, and categories of defects, graphitic domains, which lead to controversial explanations for the energy storage mechanism. Herein, self-freestanding membrane of cellulose-derived carbon fibers is presented, delivering a reversible Na-storage capacity of 330 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup> due to abundant ordered graphitic zones with enlarged interlayer spacings around 0.39 ± 0.03 nm. Benefiting from a robust solid electrolyte interphase layer rich in NaF nanocrystals, the membrane also holds a superior rate capability of 100 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup> and long-term cycling stability with capacity retention of 82.6% at 1.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> for 4000 cycles. A clear sodiation behavior of Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation into enlarged carbon graphitic layers at the slope step above 0.10 V and Na cluster evolution in the micropores at the plateau step around 0.01–0.10 V is disclosed by high-resolution element mapping microscopy, different from the accepted mechanism of Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation into graphitic layers at the plateau step.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aesr202400249-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aesr202400249-sec-0001-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>With the rapid development of economy, people's demand for energy is increasing day by day. However, the traditional fossil energy reserves are limited and cannot meet the needs of the environment and sustainable development. Fortunately, there are plenty of green energy sources such as solar, wind, and water that can be exploited in nature. The regional and time-domain heterogeneity of these green energy sources determines the need to develop a variety of new secondary battery energy storage technologies to balance regional and peak-valley differences. Among various secondary battery energy storage technologies, lithium-ion batteries have made great progress in recent years, and their energy density has been greatly improved.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202400249-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202400249-bib-0001_R_d187944529e282" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Small Sci.">1</a>-<a href="#aesr202400249-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202400249-bib-0005_R_d187944529e282" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Chem. Eng. J.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the reserves of lithium, cobalt, nickel, and other metals related to lithium-ion batteries are limited and their geographical distribution is uneven.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202400249-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202400249-bib-0006_R_d187944529e287" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Joule">6</a>-<a href="#aesr202400249-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202400249-bib-0008_R_d187944529e287" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Thanks to its abundant resource reserves and cost-effective costs, sodium-ion batteries have gradually developed into one of the more potential energy storage alternative technologies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202400249-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202400249-bib-0009_R_d187944529e294" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a>-<a href="#aesr202400249-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202400249-bib-0012_R_d187944529e294" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Energy">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Reliable positive electrode options include Prussian blue analogues, layered metal oxides, polyanionic ultrafine ion conductors, and so on.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202400249-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202400249-bib-0013_R_d187944529e299" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Sci.">13</a>-<a href="#aesr202400249-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202400249-bib-0015_R_d187944529e299" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As to the anodes, carbon stands out among various materials, e.g., alloys, organics, and layered sulfides, for its advantages of low cost, abundant microstructures, considerable sodium-storage capacity, ideal ultralow operating voltage, and so on.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202400249-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202400249-bib-0016_R_d187944529e304" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nat. Commun.">16</a>-<a href="#aesr202400249-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202400249-bib-0021_R_d187944529e304" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Chem. Eng. J.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, hard carbon and its graphite composite derivatives can not only provide ultrahigh sodium-storage capacity and good long-life cycle stability, but also obtain high initial coulomb efficiencies suitable for commercial application through reasonable optimization.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202400249-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202400249-bib-0022_R_d187944529e310" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a>-<a href="#aesr202400249-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202400249-bib-0024_R_d187944529e310" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Carbon">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, graphite-like crystals engineered by high-temperature calcination and graphite crystal templating achieved a large reversible Na-storage capacity of 321.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a high initial coulombic efficiency of 99.5%, benefiting from the enlarged lattice spacings.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202400249-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202400249-bib-0025_R_d187944529e317" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Mater. Today">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Although the electrochemical performance of hard carbon-based sodium-storage anodes has made remarkable progress in recent years, various in-situ characterization techniques play an important role in revealing the chemical process of sodium storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202400249-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202400249-bib-0026_R_d187944529e324" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nat. Energy">26</a>-<a href="#aesr202400249-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202400249-bib-0028_R_d187944529e324" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Nano">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, unlike crystalline materials, the complex structure and ion diffusion and charge transfer dynamic process of hard carbon make it extremely difficult to directly analyze its mechanism, and its controversial mechanism explanation has existed for a long time, e.g., which process is more preferred: Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation in graphitic layers, surface/interface Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption, or filling in the micropores as metallic Na clusters, as illustrated in <b>Scheme</b> <a href="#aesr202400249-fig-0001">1</a>? This disputed mechanism of sodium storage is extremely unfavorable to further reasonable design of efficient sodium-storage anode. It is still very meaningful and challenging to obtain intuitive evidence of sodiation behavior and guide the design of efficient sodium storage. Hence, based on a reported ethylene glycol-modified pyrolysis method,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202400249-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202400249-bib-0001_R_d187944529e339" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Small Sci.">1</a>, <a href="#aesr202400249-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202400249-bib-0029_R_d187944529e339" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> we presented the synthesis of self-freestanding graphitic carbon membrane, which exhibits fast kinetics in efficient Na storage. In the meantime, we try to disclose the sodiation behavior of this kind hard carbon anode by a four-probe high-resolution element mapping microscopy, providing direct clues for Na-storage mechanism.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aesr202400249-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f8dfc12c-1b61-4fb5-9dfe-fcee75e29968/aesr202400249-cstr-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f8dfc12c-1b61-4fb5-9dfe-fcee75e29968/aesr202400249-cstr-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2179bba6-c23e-4307-a829-945961820cb8/aesr202400249-cstr-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f8dfc12c-1b61-4fb5-9dfe-fcee75e29968/aesr202400249-cstr-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Scheme 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aesr202400249-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faesr.202400249" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Conventional Na-storage behavior in hard carbon, intercalation in graphitic layers, and surface/interface adsorption or filling in the micropores.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aesr202400249-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aesr202400249-sec-0002-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>As seen in the scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aesr202400249-fig-0002">1</a>), the carbon membrane inherits the fiber-like skeleton of the cellulose fiber in the roll paper, similar with ethylene glycol-modified qualitative filter paper reported previously.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202400249-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202400249-bib-0029_R_d187944529e381" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Mater.">29</a>, <a href="#aesr202400249-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202400249-bib-0030_R_d187944529e381" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Energy Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Differently, most of the skeletons are composed of curly nanoscale layers (Figure <a href="#aesr202400249-fig-0002">1a–c</a>). The uniform contrast indicates that the carbon membrane probably has a consistent composition. The SEM energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX) (Figure S1, Supporting Information) shows O doping content of 4.88% (atomic) in the curly carbon layer without any other impurities. The X-ray diffractometer (XRD) pattern (Figure S2, Supporting Information) and selected area electron diffraction image (Figure <a href="#aesr202400249-fig-0002">1d</a>) confirm that the carbon membrane possesses a graphite-2H structure (PDF No. 41-1487) with poor crystallinity. A widened reflection corresponding to an enlarged interlayer spacing around 0.39 ± 0.03 nm is observed around 21°(2<i>θ</i>) in the XRD pattern, ascribing to the diffraction of (002) crystal planes of graphite-2H structure. The graphitic structure can be further confirmed in the high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images (Figure <a href="#aesr202400249-fig-0002">1e,f</a>), appearing as disordered and nanosized graphitic zones with well-defined lattice fringes with interlayer spacings of 0.36–0.42 nm. The Raman spectra (Figure S3, Supporting Information) also display feature peaks of graphitic carbon, i.e., D band relevant to turbostratic structure in <i>sp</i><sup>2</sup> hybrid graphitic layer or <i>sp</i><sup>3</sup> hybridized carbon, and G band related to <i>sp</i><sup>2</sup> hybrid graphitic carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202400249-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202400249-bib-0031_R_d187944529e408" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These graphitic zones constructed abundant micropores and nanopores for ion storage and penetration. The existence of ordered nanopores can be verified by the peak shoulders displayed by the small-angle XRD pattern (Figure S4, Supporting Information) with pore width of 5.1, 7.7, and 12 nm, respectively. Besides, the Barrett–Joyner–Halenda (BJH) desorption pore distribution (Figure S5, Supporting Information) also displays continuous distribution of abundant pores from nanosize to microsize. But the very small adsorption quantity in N<sub>2</sub> adsorption isotherm indicated that the sample might have a very small Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface area. The nonclosed desorption branch in the isotherm is probably resulted from the very small surface area of the sample in which some surface defects and nearly closed pores would contribute to easy adsorption but very difficult desorption. Well-deconvoluted peaks of C<span class="icomoon"></span>O, OC<span class="icomoon"></span>O, C<span class="icomoon"></span>C signals in the C 1<i>s</i> X-ray photoelectron spectrometer (XPS) spectra (Figure S6, Supporting Information) illustrate the existence and manner of the O dopants with a total atomic ratio of 4.68%. The weakened absorbance band in the fourier transform infrared&nbsp;spectra (Figure S7, Supporting Information) is attributed to significantly polarization loss resulted from continuous reflection of signals by the unique graphitic zone and pore structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202400249-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202400249-bib-0032_R_d187944529e418" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Nanoscale Adv.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aesr202400249-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a91a89e5-3902-4c26-ad73-6d693ff5bb35/aesr202400249-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a91a89e5-3902-4c26-ad73-6d693ff5bb35/aesr202400249-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/192f666f-5e9e-44e5-8625-609f5f37efec/aesr202400249-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a91a89e5-3902-4c26-ad73-6d693ff5bb35/aesr202400249-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aesr202400249-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faesr.202400249" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Microstructure of the cellulose-derived carbon fibers: a,b) SEM, c) TEM, d) SAED, and e,f) HRTEM images.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Similar with other hard carbon materials, the self-freestanding carbon membrane also exhibits a two-step sodiation behavior as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aesr202400249-fig-0003">2a</a>, characteristic of a slope zone (step i) and a plateau zone (step ii) in the discharge profile. It is generally accepted that step i (the slope zone) is relevant to ion adsorption process with fast kinetics, while step ii (the plateau zone) corresponds to the intercalation and micropore filling reactions with relatively slow kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202400249-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202400249-bib-0033_R_d187944529e454" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Energy Mater.">33</a>-<a href="#aesr202400249-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202400249-bib-0035_R_d187944529e454" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Nano Lett.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The cyclic voltammogram (CV) curves (Figure <a href="#aesr202400249-fig-0003">2b</a>) under different rates of 0.2–5 mV s<sup>−1</sup> also indicate that the two steps follow the above dynamics law. As the scan rate increases, the redox peak currents increase for the process with fast kinetics, i.e., the slope region. Quite differently, the plateau region with relatively slow kinetics differs at various rates. At relatively small rates, the peak currents increase with scan rates. However, once the scan rate increases too large, e.g., here at 2 and 5 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, the process with low kinetics is severely suppressed, showing very small peaks or even disappearing due to large hysteresis. The phenomenon is also reflected in the voltage profiles (Figure <a href="#aesr202400249-fig-0003">2c</a>). At a relatively small rate of 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, a large reversible Na-storage capacity of 330 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can be achieved, in which the two steps both contribute to a half, i.e., 165 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. As the rate increases, the capacity decreases to 305 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, 250 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, 150 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, 110 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and 95 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The good rate performance (Figure <a href="#aesr202400249-fig-0003">2d</a>) benefits from not only the good electron conductive network and fabric-like ion interpenetrating skeleton of the binder-free freestanding membrane, but also the slightly lowers charge-transfer resistance after solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formation as indicated by the electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) spectra of the pristine electrode and the electrode after discharge–charge two cycles at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure S8, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aesr202400249-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8fab4ca4-c7e8-4400-8f15-7e8eaeb5862e/aesr202400249-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8fab4ca4-c7e8-4400-8f15-7e8eaeb5862e/aesr202400249-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/214425b9-9fee-4d59-88ce-cc399c4f1565/aesr202400249-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8fab4ca4-c7e8-4400-8f15-7e8eaeb5862e/aesr202400249-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aesr202400249-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faesr.202400249" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Na-storage performance of the cellulose-derived carbon fibers: a) sodiation profile, b) CVs, c) galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles and d) rate performance at different rates from 50 to 2000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, and e,f) cycling performance at 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and 1.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Except for the large Na-storage capacity and good rate performance, the membrane also shows excellent cycling stability. At a discharge rate of 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#aesr202400249-fig-0003">2e</a>), the battery stably runs for more than 500 cycles with &gt;95% capacity retention and nearly 100% coulombic efficiency. Even at a large rate of 1.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#aesr202400249-fig-0003">2f</a>), the capacity retention can be maintained at 82.6% after 4000 cycles. As described in the above, the plateau zone with relatively slow kinetic process will be largely inhibited. Almost 100% capacity retention can be obtained at ultralarge rate of 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> for more than 10 000 cycles (Figure S9, Supporting Information). Besides the intrinsic advantages of the self-freestanding membrane, the fast kinetic process of the slope zone also accounts for the excellent cycling stability under ultralarge rate.</p>
         
         <p>To get the insight into the excellent Na-storage performance, the membrane electrode under different discharge stage is characterized. The scanning transmission electron microscope (STEM) image (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aesr202400249-fig-0004">3a</a>) of the membrane electrode just after the slope process displays a clear SEI layer of about a few hundred nanometers. The SEI layer appears as a porous structure comprising abundant nanoparticles, which consist of F and Na elements in the EDX element mapping images (Figure <a href="#aesr202400249-fig-0004">3b,c</a>). The nanoparticles in the porous SEI layer were further confirmed as polycrystalline NaF, as indicated by the well-defined diffraction rings in the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) image (Figure <a href="#aesr202400249-fig-0004">3d</a>) consistent to that of cubic structure NaF (PDF no. 36-1455), indicating that the SEI layer contains abundant nanocrystals of cubic NaF. As nanocrystals of fluorides in the interphases avail the structure stability and diffusion of Na ion, the porous and robust structure of the SEI layer further explains the good cycling stability and rate performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202400249-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202400249-bib-0036_R_d187944529e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Energy Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aesr202400249-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/15346876-a176-4fac-9e03-dc89925acba4/aesr202400249-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/15346876-a176-4fac-9e03-dc89925acba4/aesr202400249-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4ded5d02-5260-4d5f-8368-ec180f402ff9/aesr202400249-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/15346876-a176-4fac-9e03-dc89925acba4/aesr202400249-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aesr202400249-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faesr.202400249" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">TEM characterization of the cellulose-derived carbon fibers after being discharged to 0.1 V at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup>: a) STEM, b,c) EDS element mapping, and d) SAED images.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To further analyze the mechanism of Na storage, the SEI layer is partial etched by high-energy electron beam (Figure S10, Supporting Information). <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aesr202400249-fig-0005">4a</a> shows the magnified STEM image of the exposure area. By carefully performing the high-resolution element mapping microscopy, the sodiation sites will be clearly identified. As shown in Figure <a href="#aesr202400249-fig-0005">4b</a>, the Na element mapping for the inserted Na characteristic of curly fringes conforming to the lattice fringes of graphitic carbon layer can be clearly identified, which are marked by blue arrows in the high-resolution element mapping image. The locally consistent Na element and graphitic layer patterns indicate the happening of Na intercalated into graphitic layers at the slope region of discharge process, differently from previous reports. The SAED image (Figure <a href="#aesr202400249-fig-0005">4c</a>) of this area also presented severely diffuse diffraction rings (vs Figure <a href="#aesr202400249-fig-0002">1d</a>), implying significantly reduced ordered structure of the graphitic layer microdomains due to intercalated Na<sup>+</sup>. The reduced ordered structure of graphitic zones can be further confirmed in the HRTEM image (Figure <a href="#aesr202400249-fig-0005">4d</a>). These evidences indicate that the slope zone in the discharge profile (0.10–1.0 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) is highly related to Na<sup>+</sup> intercalating into graphitic interlayer gaps, except for the well-known adsorption mechanism. In other words, both adsorption and intercalation processes of Na<sup>+</sup> showed fast kinetics and are associated with the lattice-enlarged graphitic zones, rather than various pore structures commonly thought to be involved.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aesr202400249-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b2e9302a-5dfc-49f7-86eb-e602e0cf16dc/aesr202400249-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b2e9302a-5dfc-49f7-86eb-e602e0cf16dc/aesr202400249-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6f99c20f-f31d-4aab-b377-4371dee85aaa/aesr202400249-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b2e9302a-5dfc-49f7-86eb-e602e0cf16dc/aesr202400249-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aesr202400249-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faesr.202400249" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">HRTEM characterization of the cellulose-derived carbon fibers after being discharged to 0.1 V at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup>: a) STEM, b) EDS element mapping, c) SAED, and d) HRTEM images of an area after strong electron beam exposure.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>As the membrane electrode is discharged to 0.01 V, an interesting phenomenon happens to the SEI layer. The STEM images (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aesr202400249-fig-0006">5a</a>) show that the initial formed porous structure of the SEI is densified. The thickness reduces to about 100–150 nm, and the distribution of F and Na elements (Figure <a href="#aesr202400249-fig-0006">5b,c</a>) is more uniform. The diffraction rings in the SAED images (Figure <a href="#aesr202400249-fig-0006">5d</a>) become finer and smoother, indicating more uniform distribution of NaF nanocrystals. In the same way, the SEI layer is excluded by high-energy electron beam (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aesr202400249-fig-0007">6a</a>). Different from the fringe-like intercalated Na at the slope zone, aggregation of Na cluster occurs in the plateau zone, as indicated by the thick spots of Na mapping sites near the graphitic zones which are partly marked by red arrows in the high-resolution element mapping image (Figure <a href="#aesr202400249-fig-0007">6b</a>). The SAED image (Figure <a href="#aesr202400249-fig-0007">6c</a>) keeps diffuse rings as that of the slope zone. More dense white spots in the bight-field HRTEM (Figure <a href="#aesr202400249-fig-0007">6d</a>) imply the aggregated Na clusters. They together point to a fact that the plateau zone (0.01–0.10 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) of the discharge process of the membrane cathode corresponds to Na reduction in the pores, forming probably Na-metal cluster.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202400249-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202400249-bib-0037_R_d187944529e685" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Chem. Commun.">37</a>, <a href="#aesr202400249-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202400249-bib-0038_R_d187944529e685" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Mater.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aesr202400249-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3643c083-3247-429a-bd6d-87208af419dc/aesr202400249-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/3643c083-3247-429a-bd6d-87208af419dc/aesr202400249-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9057f9cc-3df4-4094-9e9f-9d3b67087483/aesr202400249-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3643c083-3247-429a-bd6d-87208af419dc/aesr202400249-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aesr202400249-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faesr.202400249" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">TEM characterization of the cellulose-derived carbon fibers after being discharged to 0.001 V at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup>: a) STEM, b,c) EDS element mapping, and d) SAED images.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aesr202400249-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0e2dab86-ed49-479e-87e7-56265a5d17f2/aesr202400249-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0e2dab86-ed49-479e-87e7-56265a5d17f2/aesr202400249-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/563465ad-4ec5-4147-a34e-5d394d138266/aesr202400249-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0e2dab86-ed49-479e-87e7-56265a5d17f2/aesr202400249-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aesr202400249-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faesr.202400249" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">HRTEM characterization of the cellulose-derived carbon fibers after being discharged to 0.001 V at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup>: a) STEM, b) EDS element mapping, c) SAED, and d) HRTEM images of an area after strong electron beam exposure.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aesr202400249-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aesr202400249-sec-0003-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, a self-freestanding carbon membrane of hard carbon was presented. When used for Na storage, the membrane delivers a large ion storage capability and good cycling stability and rate performance. By means of a four-probe high-resolution EDX element microscopy, the sodiation behavior of hard carbon is reexplained, different from the well-accepted adsorption–intercalation or adsorption–pore-filling mechanisms. The newly established adsorption/intercalation–reduction mechanisms for Na storage well guide the fabrication of good Na-storage anode. For anode with fast kinetics, more lattice-enlarged graphitic zones should be designed. Overall, the work presents us direct sodiation identification of hard carbon and reasonable guidelines for designing good anodes.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aesr202400249-sec-0004">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aesr202400249-sec-0004-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aesr202400249-sec-0005">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aesr202400249-sec-0006">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aesr202400249-sec-0007">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aesr202400249-sec-0007-title"> Materials</h4>
                  
                  <p>Refer to a previous article,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202400249-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202400249-bib-0029_R_d187944529e768" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the self-freestanding membrane of cellulose-derived carbon fibers was synthesized by pyrolytically treating ethylene glycol-modified roll paper (few Ca and Mg impurities were eliminated by diluted hydrochloric acid) at 800 °C and vacuum circumstance for 6 h in a tube furnace. The ramp rate in the heating process was 10 °C min<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aesr202400249-sec-0008">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aesr202400249-sec-0008-title"> Physicochemical Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>The corresponding morphology, composition, and chemical bonding of the freestanding membrane of cellulose-derived carbon fibers were characterized by thermal field emission SEM (Quanta 400 F, 20 kV), field emission TEM (FEI Tecnai G2 F30, 300 kV), four-probe high-resolution EDX element mapping microscopy, XPS (ESCA Lab250), and Raman (Nicolet NXR 9650). Al Kα radiation was used as the X-ray source for XPS. C 1<i>s</i> at 284.6 eV was used for binding energy calibration. The laser wavelength of 532 nm was chosen as the excitation source for Raman test. Phase characterization was conducted on a powder XRD (D/MAX 2200) with Cu Kα1 radiation as the X-ray source, and the XRD pattern was taken with a scan speed of 5° min<sup>−1</sup>. The surface area and pore analysis were carried out on a Micromeritics instrument (ASAP 2420) with N<sub>2</sub> as adsorbate at 77 K. The specific surface area and pore distribution were evaluated based on BET-specific surface and BJH desorption pore.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aesr202400249-sec-0009">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aesr202400249-sec-0009-title"> Electrochemical Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>For electrochemical characterization, the self-freestanding membrane of cellulose-derived carbon fibers with the weight of 0.6–0.8 mg cm<sup>−2</sup> for a single layer was used directly as the working electrodes. The weight of all the membrane electrodes was confirmed by a microbalance with the precision of 0.1 μg. Na plates were used as both the working and counter electrodes. The Whatman glass fiber microfilter was used as the separator. 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in ethylene carbonate (EC)/dimethyl carbonate (DMC)/ethyl–methyl carbonate (EMC) (v/v/v = 1:1:1) and 5% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC, wt%) was used as the electrolyte. Standard cells (CR2032) with the carbon membrane cathode, Na plate anode, glass microfiber filter separator, and 75 μL electrolyte were assembled in an Ar-filled universal glove box with oxygen and water content less than 0.3 ppm.</p>
                  
                  <p>The batteries were galvanostatically charged and discharged in a voltage cutoff of 0.01–2 V at various rates from 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup> to 10 Ag<sup>−1</sup> on a multichannel Neware battery testing system. The CV scanning at varying rates from 0.2 to 5 mVs<sup>−1</sup> in voltage cutoff of 0–2 V and EIS scanning from 1 MHz to 0.01 Hz with an ac signal amplitude of 10 mV were performed on an Ivium electrochemical workstation. Batteries for mechanism analysis were galvanostatically discharged and charged to different cutoff voltages at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. All the electrodes for characterization were washed with DEC or DMC to eliminate residual electrolytes.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
